<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History of Europe</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1965" data-file-width="2691" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg/310px-Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg/465px-Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg/620px-Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Ortelius_Map_of_Europe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe by cartographer <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> in 1595</div></div></div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p>The <b>history of Europe</b> is traditionally divided into four time periods: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Europe" title="Prehistoric Europe">prehistoric Europe</a> (prior to about 800 BC), <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> (800 BC to AD 500), the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> (AD 500 to AD 1500), and the <a href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">modern era</a> (since AD 1500).
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Early_European_modern_humans" title="Early European modern humans">early European modern humans</a> appear in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a> about 48,000 years ago, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleolithic_Era" title="Paleolithic Era">Paleolithic Era</a>. People from this period left behind numerous artifacts, including works of art, burial sites, and tools, allowing some reconstruction of their society. During the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a>, Europe saw migrations from the east and southeast. Settled agriculture marked the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_Era" title="Neolithic Era">Neolithic Era</a>, which spread slowly across Europe from southeast to the north and west. The later Neolithic period saw the introduction of early <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> and the use of copper-based tools and weapons, and the building of <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megalithic</a> structures, as exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>.
</p><p>The period known as <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> began with the emergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_state" title="City state">city-states</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>. Some of the earliest examples of <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> come from the writings of the ancient Greeks, such as <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> came to dominate the entire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_basin" title="Mediterranean basin">Mediterranean basin</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic people</a> began in the late 4th century AD and made gradual incursions into various parts of the Roman Empire. As these migratory people settled down and formed state societies of their own, this marked the transition period out of the classical era.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">The Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> in AD 476 traditionally marks the start of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. While the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> would persist for another 1000 years, the former lands of the Western Empire would be fragmented into a number of different states. At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">early Slavs</a> began to become established as a distinct group in the central and eastern parts of Europe. The first great empire of the Middle Ages was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Islamic conquest of Iberia</a> established <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a> saw a second great migration of <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> peoples. Attempts to retake the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> from the Muslim states that occupied it made the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> the age of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Crusades" title="The Crusades">The Crusades</a>, while the political system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> came to its height. The <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a> were marked by large population declines, as Europe was threatened by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bubonic_Plague" title="Bubonic Plague">Bubonic Plague</a>, as well as invasions by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> peoples from the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a>. At the end of the Middle Ages, there was a transitional period, known as the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Europe" title="Early Modern Europe">Early Modern Europe</a> is usually dated to the end of the 15th century. Technological changes such as <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> changed how warfare was conducted and how knowledge was preserved and disseminated. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> saw the fragmentation of religious thought, leading to <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">religious wars</a>.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Exploration" title="Age of Exploration">Age of Exploration</a> led to <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a>, and the exploitation of the people and resources of colonies brought resources and wealth to Europe.  After 1800, the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> accumulation and rapid <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> to Western Europe, while several countries transitioned away from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_absolutism" title="Age of absolutism">absolutist rule</a> to parliamentary regimes. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Revolutions" title="Age of Revolutions">Age of Revolutions</a> saw long-established political systems upset and turned over. In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> led to a remaking of the map of Europe as the large Empires were broken up into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation-state" title="Nation-state">nation-states</a>. Lingering political issues would lead to <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, during which <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> perpetrated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>. After World War II, during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, most of Europe became divided by the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a> in two military blocs: <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>. The post-war period saw <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonization</a> as Western European colonial empires were dismantled.  The post-war period also featured the gradual development of the <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> process, which led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>; this extended to Eastern European countries after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall" title="Fall of the Berlin Wall">Fall of the Berlin Wall</a>. The 21st century saw the <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">European debt crisis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the European Union</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1372" data-file-width="3326" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/290px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/435px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/580px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indo-European migrations spread <a href="/wiki/Yamnaya_culture" title="Yamnaya culture">Yamnaya</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_Steppe_Herders" title="Western Steppe Herders">Steppe pastoralist</a> ancestry and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a> across large parts of Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Europe" title="Neolithic Europe">Neolithic era</a> (starting at c. 7000 BC.) and the time of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a> (starting at c. 4000 BC.) Europe saw massive migrations from east and southeast which also brought agriculture, new technologies, and the Indo-European languages, primarily through the areas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan_peninsula" title="Balkan peninsula">Balkan peninsula</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_sea" title="Black sea">Black sea</a> region.
</p><p>Some of the best-known civilizations of the late prehistoric Europe were the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a>, which flourished during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> until they <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">collapsed</a> in a short period of time around 1200 BC.
</p><p>The period known as <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> began with the emergence of the city-states of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>. After ultimately checking the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian</a> advance in Europe through the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a> in the 5th century BC, Greek influence reached its zenith under the expansive empire of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, spreading throughout Asia, Africa, and other parts of Europe.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a>, their powerful <a href="/wiki/Odrysian_kingdom" title="Odrysian kingdom">Odrysian kingdom</a>, distinct <a href="/wiki/Thracian_treasure" title="Thracian treasure">culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thracian_Tomb_of_Sveshtari" title="Thracian Tomb of Sveshtari">architecture</a> were long present in Southeast Europe.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1619" data-file-width="2187" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png/220px-Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png/330px-Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png/440px-Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spread_of_Christianity_to_AD_600_-_Atlas_of_World_History.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>History of the <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Christianity" title="Spread of Christianity">spread of Christianity</a>: in 325 AD (dark blue) and 600 AD (blue).</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> came to dominate the entire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_basin" title="Mediterranean basin">Mediterranean basin</a>. By 300 AD the Roman Empire was divided into the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern</a> empires. During the 4th and 5th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> of Northern Europe, pressed by the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, grew in strength and led repeated attacks that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>. The Western empire's collapse in AD 476 traditionally marks the end of the classical period and the start of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.
</p><p>In Western Europe, Germanic peoples became more powerful in the remnants of the former Western Roman Empire and established kingdoms and empires of their own. Of all of the Germanic peoples, the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> would rise to a position of <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> over Western Europe, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> reaching its peak under <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> around 800. This empire was later divided into several parts; <a href="/wiki/West_Francia" title="West Francia">West Francia</a> would evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">Kingdom of France</a>, while <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a> would evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, a precursor to modern Germany and Italy. The <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> were the site of several large-scale migrations.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> – the eastern part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, with its capital <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, survived for the next 1000 years. During most of its existence, the empire was the most dominant <a href="/wiki/List_of_empires" title="List of empires">empire</a>, also most powerful economic, cultural, and military force in Europe. The powerful and long lived <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian Empire</a> was its main competitor in the region of Southeast Europe for centuries. Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">architecture</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">political dominance</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_medieval_Bulgarian_culture" title="Golden Age of medieval Bulgarian culture">Bulgarian cultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">linguistic</a> achievements left great legacy in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> Europe and beyond through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> to this day.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, a period of migrations of <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Scandinavian</a> peoples, occurred from the late 8th century to the middle 11th century. The <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>, descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> who settled in Northern France, had a significant impact on many parts of Europe, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_Sicily" title="Norman conquest of Sicily">Sicily</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus' people</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, which evolved into Russia. After 1000 the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> were a series of religiously motivated military expeditions originally intended to bring the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> back under Christian rule. The Crusaders opened trade routes which enabled the merchant republics of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> to become major economic powers. The <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a>, a related movement, worked to reconquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> for <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2850" data-file-width="2460" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg/220px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg/330px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg/440px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_octobre_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The peasants preparing the fields for the winter with a <a href="/wiki/Harrow_(tool)" title="Harrow (tool)">harrow</a> and sowing for the winter grain, from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Very_Rich_Hours_of_the_Duke_of_Berry" title="The Very Rich Hours of the Duke of Berry">The Very Rich Hours of the Duke of Berry</a></i>, c.1410</div></div></div>
<p>Eastern Europe in the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> was dominated by the rise and fall of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>. Led by <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, the Mongols were a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steppe_nomad" title="Steppe nomad">steppe nomads</a> who established a decentralized empire which, at its height, extended from China in the east to the Black and Baltic Seas in Europe. As Mongol power waned towards the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a> rose to become the strongest of the numerous Russian principalities and republics and would grow into the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a> in 1547. The Late Middle Ages represented a period of upheaval in Europe. The epidemic known as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> and an associated famine caused demographic catastrophe in Europe as the population plummeted. Dynastic struggles and wars of conquest kept many of the states of Europe at war for much of the period. In Scandinavia, the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> dominated the political landscape, while England fought with Scotland in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a> and with France in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>. In Central Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> became a large territorial empire, while the Holy Roman Empire, which was an <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">elective monarchy</a>, came to be dominated for centuries by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">House of Habsburg</a>. Russia continued to expand southward and eastward into former Mongol lands. In the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> overran Byzantine lands, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453, which historians mark as the end of the Middle Ages.
</p><p>Beginning in the 14th century in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> and later spreading through Europe, a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> of knowledge challenged traditional doctrines in science and theology. The rediscovery of classical Greek and Roman knowledge had an enormous liberating effect on intellectuals. Simultaneously, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> under German <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> questioned Papal authority. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> seized control of the English Church and its lands. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_religious_wars" title="European religious wars">European religious wars</a> were fought between German and Spanish rulers. The <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> ended Muslim rule in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a>. By the 1490s a series of oceanic explorations marked the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, establishing direct links with Africa, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Americas" title="The Americas">the Americas</a>, and Asia. Religious wars continued to be fought in Europe, until the 1648 <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_empire" title="Spanish empire">Spanish crown</a> maintained its hegemony in Europe and was the leading power on the continent until the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_the_Pyrenees" title="Treaty of the Pyrenees">Treaty of the Pyrenees</a>, which ended a conflict between Spain and France that had begun during the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>. An unprecedented series of major wars and political revolutions took place around Europe and the world in the period between 1610 and 1700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/220px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/330px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/440px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">Watt steam engine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>, fuelled primarily by coal, propelled the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> in 19th-century Northwestern Europe.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> began in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>, based on coal, steam, and textile mills. Political change in <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> was spurred by the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> under the motto <i><a href="/wiki/Libert%C3%A9,_%C3%A9galit%C3%A9,_fraternit%C3%A9" title="Liberté, égalité, fraternité">liberté, égalité, fraternité</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> took control, made many reforms inside France, and transformed Western Europe. But his rise stimulated both nationalism and reaction and he was defeated in 1814–15 as the old royal conservatives returned to power.
</p><p>The period between 1815 and 1871 saw revolutionary attempts in much of Europe (apart from Britain). They all failed however. As industrial work forces grew in Western Europe, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> and trade union activity developed. The last vestiges of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a> were abolished <a href="/wiki/Serfdom_in_Russia" title="Serfdom in Russia">in Russia</a> in 1861. Greece and the other <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan nations</a> began a long slow road to <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Balkans" title="History of the Balkans">independence</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, starting in the 1820s. Italy was unified in its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Risorgimento" title="Risorgimento">Risorgimento</a> in 1860. After the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> of 1870–71, <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> unified the German states into an empire that was politically and militarily dominant until 1914. Most of Europe scrambled for imperial colonies <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">in Africa</a> and Asia in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Empire" title="The Age of Empire">the Age of Empire</a>. Britain and France built the largest empires, while diplomats ensured there were no major wars in Europe, apart from the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of the 1850s.
</p><p>The outbreak of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> in 1914 was precipitated by the rise of nationalism in Southeastern Europe as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Powers" title="Great Powers">Great Powers</a> took sides. The 1917 <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> led the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> to become the world's first <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">communist state</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>, led by Britain, France, and the United States, defeated the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>, led by the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, in 1918. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Big_Four_(World_War_I)" title="The Big Four (World War I)">Big Four</a> imposed their terms in a series of treaties, especially the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>. The war's human and material devastation was unprecedented.
</p><p>Germany lost its overseas empire and several provinces, had to pay large reparations, and was humiliated by the victors. They in turn had large debts to the United States. The 1920s were prosperous until 1929 when the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> broke out, which led to the collapse of democracy in many European states. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> regime under <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> came to power in 1933, rearmed Germany, and along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">Mussolini's Italy</a> sought to assert themselves on the continent. Other nations, who had not taken to the attractions of fascism, sought to avoid conflict. They set boundaries of <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a>, which Hitler continually ignored. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> began. The war ended with the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> but the threat of more conflict was recognised before the war's end. Many from the US were suspicious of how the USSR would treat the peace – in the USSR there was paranoia at US forces in Europe. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a>/<a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Western Front (World War II)">Western Front</a> meetings among leaders in <a href="/wiki/Yalta" title="Yalta">Yalta</a> proved inconclusive. In the closing months of the war there was a race to the finish. The territories liberated from the Nazis by troops from the USSR found they had exchanged fascism for socialism. The USSR, however, would not leave those territories for forty years. The USSR claimed they needed buffer states between them and the nascent <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>. In the west, the term <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a> entered the language. The United States launched the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> from 1948 to 1951 and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> from 1949, and rebuilt industrial economies that all were thriving by the 1950s. France and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> took the lead in forming the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, which eventually became the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU). Secularization saw the weakening of Protestant and Catholic churches across most of Europe, except where they were symbols of reaction, as in Poland. The <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">Revolutions of 1989</a> brought an end to both Soviet hegemony and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> in Eastern Europe, the resulting capitalist restoration engendering economic and social devastation for the people. Germany was reunited, <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Europe's integration deepened</a>, and both NATO and the EU expanded to the east. The EU came under increasing pressure because of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">worldwide recession after 2008</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_of_Europe">Prehistory of Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory of Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Europe" title="Prehistoric Europe">Prehistoric Europe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeolithic_Europe" title="Palaeolithic Europe">Palaeolithic Europe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesolithic_Europe" title="Mesolithic Europe">Mesolithic Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Europe" title="Neolithic Europe">Neolithic Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Europe" title="Bronze Age Europe">Bronze Age Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Europe" title="Iron Age Europe">Iron Age Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Early_European_modern_humans" title="Early European modern humans">Early European modern humans</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Paleolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_Prehistory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="1361" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Europe_Prehistory.gif/250px-Europe_Prehistory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Europe_Prehistory.gif/375px-Europe_Prehistory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Europe_Prehistory.gif/500px-Europe_Prehistory.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_Prehistory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map depicting the earliest human migration in prehistoric Europe.</div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> migrated from Africa to Europe before the emergence of modern humans. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_erectus_georgicus" title="Homo erectus georgicus">Homo erectus georgicus</a></i>, which lived roughly 1.8 million years ago in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, is the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid</a> to have been discovered in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9zignan-la-C%C3%A8be" title="Lézignan-la-Cèbe">Lézignan-la-Cèbe</a> in France, <a href="/wiki/Orce" title="Orce">Orce</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> in Spain, <a href="/wiki/Monte_Poggiolo" title="Monte Poggiolo">Monte Poggiolo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic.it_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic.it-5">[5]</a></sup> in Italy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kozarnika" title="Kozarnika">Kozarnika</a> in Bulgaria are among the oldest Palaeolithic sites in Europe.
</p><p>The earliest appearance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern people</a> in Europe has been dated to 45,000 BC, referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Early_European_modern_humans" title="Early European modern humans">Early European modern humans</a>. The earliest sites in Europe are
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riparo_Mochi" title="Riparo Mochi">Riparo Mochi</a> (Italy), <a href="/wiki/Geissenkl%C3%B6sterle" title="Geissenklösterle">Geissenklösterle</a> (Germany), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isturitz" title="Isturitz">Isturitz</a> (France).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
Some locally developed transitional cultures (<a href="/wiki/Uluzzian" title="Uluzzian">Uluzzian</a> in Italy and Greece, Altmühlian in Germany, <a href="/wiki/Szeletian" title="Szeletian">Szeletian</a> in Central Europe and <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2telperronian" title="Châtelperronian">Châtelperronian</a> in the southwest) use clearly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Palaeolithic" title="Upper Palaeolithic">Upper Palaeolithic</a> technologies at very early dates.
</p><p>Nevertheless, the definitive advance of these technologies is made by the <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> culture. The origins of this culture can be located in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> (Ahmarian) and Hungary (first full Aurignacian). By 35,000 BC, the Aurignacian culture and its technology had extended through most of Europe. The last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> seem to have been forced to retreat during this process to the southern half of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.
</p><p>Around 29,000 BC a new technology/culture appeared in the western region of Europe: the <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a>. This technology/culture has been theorised to have come with migrations of people from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kozarnika" title="Kozarnika">Kozarnika</a>).
</p><p>Around 16,000 BC, Europe witnessed the appearance of a new culture, known as <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a>, possibly rooted in the old Gravettian. This culture soon superseded the <a href="/wiki/Solutrean" title="Solutrean">Solutrean</a> area and the Gravettian of mainly France, Spain, Germany, Italy, Poland, Portugal and Ukraine. The <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_culture" title="Hamburg culture">Hamburg culture</a> prevailed in Northern Europe in the 14th and the 13th millennium BC as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creswellian" title="Creswellian">Creswellian</a> (also termed the British Late Magdalenian) did shortly after in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>.
Around 12,500 BC, the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrm_glaciation" title="Würm glaciation">Würm glaciation</a> ended. Slowly, through the following millennia, temperatures and sea levels rose, changing the environment of prehistoric people. Nevertheless, Magdalenian culture persisted until c. 10,000 BC, when it quickly evolved into two <i><a href="/wiki/Microlith" title="Microlith">microlithist</a></i> cultures: <a href="/wiki/Azilian" title="Azilian">Azilian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federmesser" title="Federmesser">Federmesser</a>), in Spain and <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Sauveterrian" title="Sauveterrian">Sauveterrian</a>, in southern France and <a href="/wiki/Tardenoisian" title="Tardenoisian">Tardenoisian</a> in Central Europe, while in Northern Europe the <a href="/wiki/Lyngby_culture" title="Lyngby culture">Lyngby complex</a> succeeded the Hamburg culture with the influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federmesser" title="Federmesser">Federmesser</a> group as well.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Neolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evidence of permanent settlement dates from the 8th millennium BC in the Balkans. The <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> reached <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> in the 6th millennium BC and parts of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a> in the 5th and 4th millenniums BC. The modern indigenous populations of Europe are largely descended from three distinct lineages: Mesolithic <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, a derivative of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnon" title="Cro-Magnon">Cro-Magnon</a> population of Europe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_European_farmers" title="Early European farmers">early European farmers</a> who migrated from Anatolia during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yamnaya_culture" title="Yamnaya culture">Yamnaya</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_Steppe_Herders" title="Western Steppe Herders">pastoralists</a> who expanded into Europe in the context of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The genetic makeup of speakers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uralic_language_family" title="Uralic language family">Uralic</a> language family in northern Europe was shaped by migration from <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> that began at least 3,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a> started at around c. 4200 BC. through the areas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_sea" title="Black sea">Black sea</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan_peninsula" title="Balkan peninsula">Balkan peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">East</a> and Southeast Europe. In the next 3000 years the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a> expanded through Europe. Around this time, in 4700 – 4200 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Solnitsata" title="Solnitsata">Solnitsata</a> town, believed to be the oldest prehistoric town in Europe, flourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maugh_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maugh-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Varna_Necropolis" title="Varna Necropolis">Varna Necropolis</a> – a burial site from 4569 to 4340 BC and one of the most important archaeological sites in world prehistory, was found the oldest gold treasure (elaborated golden objects) in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Niece_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-La_Niece-11">[11]</a></sup> Recently discovered golden artifacts in another site in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> near <a href="/wiki/Durankulak" title="Durankulak">Durankulak</a> appear to be 7,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Several prehistoric Bulgarian finds are considered no less old – the golden treasures of Hotnitsa, artifacts from the Kurgan settlement of Yunatsite near <a href="/wiki/Pazardzhik" title="Pazardzhik">Pazardzhik</a>, the golden treasure Sakar, as well as beads and gold jewelry found in the Kurgan settlement of <a href="/wiki/Provadia" title="Provadia">Provadia</a> – <a href="/wiki/Solnitsata" title="Solnitsata">Solnitsata</a> (“salt pit”). However, Varna gold is most often called the oldest since this treasure is the largest and most diverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

Europe.</div></div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Europe">Ancient Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ancient Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Minoans_and_Mycenae_2000.E2.80.931100_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Minoans_and_Mycenae_2000–1100_BC">Minoans and Mycenae 2000–1100 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Minoans and Mycenae 2000–1100 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1001" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg/170px-Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg/255px-Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg/340px-Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tholos_of_Atreus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Treasury of <a href="/wiki/Atreus" title="Atreus">Atreus</a>, or Tomb of Agamemnon in Mycenae 1250 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The first well-known literate civilization in Europe was that of the Minoans. The <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> civilization that arose on the island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> and flourished from approximately the 27th century BC to the 15th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It was rediscovered at the beginning of the 20th century through the work of the British archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Evans" title="Arthur Evans">Arthur Evans</a>. <a href="/wiki/Will_Durant" title="Will Durant">Will Durant</a> referred to it as "the first link in the European chain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Minoans were replaced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a> which flourished during the period roughly between 1600 BC, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helladic" title="Helladic">Helladic</a> culture in <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Greece" title="Geography of Greece">mainland Greece</a> was transformed under influences from Minoan Crete, and 1100 BC. The major Mycenaean cities were <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> in Argolis, <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> in Messenia, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> in Attica, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Thebes_(Boeotia)" title="Ancient Thebes (Boeotia)">Thebes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Boeotia)" title="Orchomenus (Boeotia)">Orchomenus</a> in Boeotia, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iolkos" title="Iolkos">Iolkos</a> in Thessaly. In <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, the Mycenaeans occupied <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>. Mycenaean settlement sites also appeared in <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> on islands in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, on the coast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Mycenaean artefacts have been found well outside the limits of the Mycenean world.
</p><p>Quite unlike the Minoans, whose society benefited from trade, the Mycenaeans advanced through conquest. Mycenaean civilization was dominated by a warrior <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>. Around 1400 BC, the Mycenaeans extended their control to Crete, the centre of the Minoan civilization, and adopted a form of the Minoan script (called <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">Linear A</a>) to write their early form of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>.
</p><p>The Mycenaean civilization perished with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">collapse of Bronze-Age civilization</a> on the eastern shores of the Mediterranean Sea. The collapse is commonly attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a>, although other theories describing natural disasters and climate change have been advanced as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Whatever the causes, the Mycenaean civilization had definitely disappeared after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LH_IIIC" title="LH IIIC">LH III C</a>, when the sites of Mycenae and Tirynth were again destroyed and lost their importance. This end, during the last years of the 12th century BC, occurred after a slow decline of the Mycenaean civilization, which lasted many years before dying out. The beginning of the 11th century BC opened a new context, that of the protogeometric, the beginning of the geometric period, the <i><a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a></i> of traditional historiography.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_antiquity_period">Early antiquity period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early antiquity period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthenon_from_south.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3519" data-file-width="2345" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Parthenon_from_south.jpg/220px-Parthenon_from_south.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Parthenon_from_south.jpg/330px-Parthenon_from_south.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Parthenon_from_south.jpg/440px-Parthenon_from_south.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parthenon_from_south.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">ancient Athenian</a> Temple on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">Acropolis</a> (hill-top city) fell to Rome in 176 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Greeks and the Romans left a legacy in Europe which is evident in <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">thought</a>, visual arts and law. Ancient Greece was a collection of city-states, out of which the original form of democracy developed. <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a> was the most powerful and developed city, and a cradle of learning from the time of <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>. Citizens' forums debated and legislated policy of the state, and from here arose some of the most notable classical philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, the last of whom taught <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.
</p><p>Through his military campaigns, the king of the kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>, Alexander, spread Hellenistic culture and learning to the banks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Indus" title="River Indus">River Indus</a>. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> strengthened through victory over <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>. Greek wisdom passed into Roman institutions, as Athens itself was absorbed under the banner of the Senate and People of Rome (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senatus_Populusque_Romanus" title="Senatus Populusque Romanus">SPQR</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe-In-301BC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1670" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Europe-In-301BC.png/220px-Europe-In-301BC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Europe-In-301BC.png/330px-Europe-In-301BC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Europe-In-301BC.png/440px-Europe-In-301BC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe-In-301BC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe in the year 301 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Romans expanded their domains from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Anatolia" title="Classical Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in the east to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a> in the west. In 44 BC as it approached its height, its dictator <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Assassination of Julius Caesar">murdered</a> by senators in an attempt to restore the Republic. In the ensuing turmoil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavian" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> (ruled as Augustus; and as <i><a href="/wiki/Divi_filius" title="Divi filius">divi filius</a></i>, or <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a>, as Julius had adopted him as an heir) usurped the reins of power and fought the Roman Senate. While proclaiming the rebirth of the Republic, he had ushered in the transfer of the Roman state from a republic to an empire, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, which lasted for nearly 15 centuries until the fall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenic_civilisation" title="Hellenic civilisation">Hellenic civilisation</a> was a collection of city-states or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poleis" title="Poleis">poleis</a> with different governments and cultures that achieved notable developments in government, philosophy, science, mathematics, politics, sports, theatre and music.
</p><p>The most powerful city-states were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sparta" title="History of Sparta">Sparta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>. Athens was a powerful Hellenic city-state and governed itself with an early form of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>; the citizens of Athens voted on legislation and executive bills themselves. Athens was the home of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a>.
</p><p>
The Hellenic city-states established colonies on the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and the Mediterranean Sea (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_Minor" title="Asian Minor">Asian Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>). By the late 6th century BC, all the Greek city states in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia-Minor" title="Asia-Minor">Asia Minor</a> had been incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a>, while the latter had made territorial gains in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paeonia_(kingdom)" title="Paeonia (kingdom)">Paeonia</a>, etc.) and Eastern Europe proper as well. In the course of the 5th century BC, some of the Greek city states attempted to overthrow Persian rule in the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a>, which failed. This sparked the <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">first Persian invasion of mainland Greece</a>. At some point during the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>, namely during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a>, and precisely after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Battle of Thermopylae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">Battle of Artemisium</a>, almost all of Greece to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a> had been overrun by the Persians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warfare_in_the_Ancient_World_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warfare_in_the_Ancient_World-26">[26]</a></sup> but the Greek city states reached a decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>. With the end of the Greco-Persian wars, the Persians were eventually decisively forced to withdraw from their territories in Europe. The Greco-Persian Wars and the victory of the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_states" title="City states">city states</a> directly influenced the entire further course of European history and would set its further tone.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2766" data-file-width="3905" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/220px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/330px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/440px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mosaic" title="Alexander Mosaic">mosaic</a> showing <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> battling <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a></div></div></div><p> Some Greek city-states formed the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a> to continue fighting Persia, but Athens' position as leader of this league led Sparta to form the rival <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_League" title="Peloponnesian League">Peloponnesian League</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian Wars</a> ensued, and the Peloponnesian League was victorious. Subsequently, discontent with <a href="/wiki/Spartan_hegemony" title="Spartan hegemony">Spartan hegemony</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">Corinthian War</a> and the defeat of Sparta at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leuctra" title="Battle of Leuctra">Battle of Leuctra</a>. At the same time at the north ruled the Thracian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odrysian_Kingdom" title="Odrysian Kingdom">Odrysian Kingdom</a> between the 5th century BC and the 1st century AD.
</p><p>Hellenic infighting left Greek city states vulnerable, and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> united the Greek city states under his control. The son of Philip II, known as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, invaded neighboring <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persia</a>, toppled and incorporated its domains, as well as invading Egypt and going as far off as India, increasing contact with people and cultures in these regions that marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">death of Alexander the Great</a>, his empire split into multiple kingdoms ruled by his generals, the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>. The Diadochi fought against each other in a series of conflicts called the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">Wars of the Diadochi</a>. In the beginning of the 2nd century BC, only three major kingdoms remained: the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a>. These kingdoms spread <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> to regions as far away as <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/220px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/330px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/440px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_republic" title="Roman republic">Roman republic</a> and its neighbours in 58 BC.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Rome">Ancient Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ancient Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_rise_of_Rome">The rise of Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The rise of Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina,_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/220px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/330px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/440px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina,_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> addresses the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a> to denounce <a href="/wiki/Catiline" title="Catiline">Catiline</a>'s conspiracy to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republic</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Maccari" title="Cesare Maccari">Cesare Maccari</a></div></div></div>
<p>Much of Greek learning was assimilated by the nascent Roman state as it expanded outward from Italy, taking advantage of its enemies' inability to unite: the only challenge to Roman ascent came from the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> colony of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, and its defeats in the three <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a> marked the start of Roman <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a>. First governed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_kings" title="Roman kings">kings</a>, then as a senatorial republic (the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>), Rome finally became an empire at the end of the 1st century BC, under <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> and his authoritarian successors.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe-In-117AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1670" data-file-width="2862" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Europe-In-117AD.png/220px-Europe-In-117AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Europe-In-117AD.png/330px-Europe-In-117AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Europe-In-117AD.png/440px-Europe-In-117AD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe-In-117AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman Empire at its greatest extent in 117 AD, under the emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> had its centre in the Mediterranean, controlling all the countries on its shores; the northern border was marked by the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> rivers. Under the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (2nd century AD) the empire reached its maximum expansion, controlling approximately 5,900,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,300,000 sq mi) of land surface, including <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gallia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquitania" title="Aquitania">Aquitania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baetica" title="Baetica">Baetica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odrysian_kingdom" title="Odrysian kingdom">Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dacia_(Roman_province)" title="Dacia (Roman province)">Dacia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>, Egypt, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, North Africa, <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a>, a period of peace, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_civilisation" title="Roman civilisation">civilisation</a> and an efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centralised_government" title="Centralised government">centralised government</a> in the subject territories ended in the 3rd century, when a <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">series of civil wars</a> undermined Rome's economic and social strength.
</p><p>In the 4th century, the emperors <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Constantine I of the Roman Empire">Constantine</a> were able to slow down the process of decline by splitting the empire into a Western part with a capital in Rome and an Eastern part with the capital in Byzantium, or <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> (now Istanbul).  <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> is generally considered to be the center of "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox civilization</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Whereas Diocletian severely persecuted <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, Constantine declared an official end to state-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution of Christians</a> in 313 with the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a>, thus setting the stage for the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Church</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church of the Roman Empire</a> in about 380.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire">Decline of the Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Decline of the Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1414" data-file-width="2052" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png/220px-Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png/330px-Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png/440px-Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Partition_of_the_Roman_Empire_in_395_AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the partition of the Roman Empire in 395, at the death of <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> is shown in red and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> (Byzantine Empire) is shown in purple</div></div></div>
