<p>The Roman Empire had been repeatedly attacked by invading armies from Northern Europe and in 476, Rome finally fell. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustus" title="Romulus Augustus">Romulus Augustus</a>, the last emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, surrendered to the Germanic King <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a>. The British historian <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> argued in <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_the_Decline_and_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire">The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire</a></i> (1776) that the Romans had become decadent and had lost civic virtue.
</p><p>Gibbon said that the adoption of Christianity meant belief in a better life after death, and therefore made people lazy and indifferent to the present. "From the eighteenth century onward", Glen W. Bowersock has remarked,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> "we have been obsessed with the fall: it has been valued as an archetype for every perceived decline, and, hence, as a symbol for our own fears." It remains one of the greatest historical questions, and has a tradition rich in scholarly interest.
</p><p>Some other notable dates are the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a> in 378, the death of <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> in 395 (the last time the Roman Empire was politically unified), the crossing of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> in 406 by <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a> after the withdrawal of the legions to defend Italy against <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a>, the death of <a href="/wiki/Stilicho" title="Stilicho">Stilicho</a> in 408, followed by the disintegration of the western legions, the death of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>, the last Roman emperor who tried to reconquer the west, in 565, and the <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">coming of Islam</a> after 632. Many scholars maintain that rather than a "fall", the changes can more accurately be described as a complex transformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Over time many theories have been proposed on why the Empire fell, or whether indeed it fell at all.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Antiquity_and_Migration_Period">Late Antiquity and Migration Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Late Antiquity and Migration Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1382" data-file-width="1954" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/220px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/330px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/440px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified map of migrations from the 2nd to the 5th century. See also the <a href="/wiki/File:World_820.png" title="File:World 820.png">map of the world in 820 AD</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>When Emperor Constantine had reconquered Rome under the banner of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a> in 312, he soon afterwards issued the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> in 313 (preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Serdica" title="Edict of Serdica">Edict of Serdica</a> in 311), declaring the legality of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in the Roman Empire. In addition, Constantine officially shifted the capital of the Roman Empire from Rome to the Greek town of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, which he renamed Nova Roma – it was later named <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> ("City of Constantine").
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>, who had made Christianity the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">official religion of the Roman Empire</a>, would be the last emperor to preside over a united Roman Empire, until his death in 395. The empire was split into two-halves: the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> centred in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, and the Eastern Roman Empire (later to be referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>) centred in Constantinople. The Roman Empire was repeatedly attacked by <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Hunnic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_people" title="Slavic people">Slavic</a> and other "barbarian" tribes (see: <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>), and in 476 finally the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western part</a> fell to the <a href="/wiki/Heruli" title="Heruli">Heruli</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa_in_526.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="799" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Europa_in_526.png/220px-Europa_in_526.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Europa_in_526.png/330px-Europa_in_526.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Europa_in_526.png/440px-Europa_in_526.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europa_in_526.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing Europe in 526 AD with the three dominating powers of the west</div></div></div>
<p>Roman authority in the Western part of the empire had collapsed, and a power vacuum left in the wake of this collapse; the central organization, institutions, laws and power of Rome had broken down, resulting in many areas being open to invasion by migrating tribes. Over time, <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a> arose, two interlocking institutions that provided for division of land and labour, as well as a broad if uneven hierarchy of law and protection. These localised hierarchies were based on the bond of common people to the land on which they worked, and to a lord, who would provide and administer both local law to settle disputes among the peasants, as well as protection from outside invaders. Unlike under Roman rule, with its standard laws and military across the empire and its great bureaucracy to administer them and collect taxes, each lord (although having obligations to a higher lord) was largely sovereign in his domain. A peasant's lot could vary greatly depending on the leadership skills and attitudes to justice of the lord toward his people. <a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">Tithes</a> or rents were paid to the lord, who in turn owed resources, and armed men in times of war, to his lord, perhaps a regional prince. However, the levels of hierarchy were varied over time and place.
</p><p>The western provinces soon were to be dominated by three great powers: first, the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> (<a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingian dynasty</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> 481–843 AD, which covered much of present France and Germany; second, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigothic_kingdom" title="Visigothic kingdom">Visigothic kingdom</a> 418–711 AD in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> (modern Spain); and third, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostrogothic_kingdom" title="Ostrogothic kingdom">Ostrogothic kingdom</a> 493–553 AD in Italy and parts of the western <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The Ostrogoths were later replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Lombards" title="Kingdom of the Lombards">Kingdom of the Lombards</a> 568–774 AD. These new powers of the west built upon the Roman traditions until they evolved into a synthesis of Roman and Germanic cultures. Although these powers covered large territories, they did not have the great resources and bureaucracy of the Roman empire to control regions and localities. The ongoing invasions and boundary disputes usually meant a more risky and varying life than that under the empire. This meant that in general more power and responsibilities were left to local lords. On the other hand, it also meant more freedom, particularly in more remote areas.
</p><p>In Italy, <a href="/wiki/Theodoric_the_Great" title="Theodoric the Great">Theodoric the Great</a> began the cultural romanisation of the new world he had constructed. He made <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> a centre of Romano-Greek culture of art and his court fostered a flowering of literature and philosophy in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>. In Iberia, <a href="/wiki/Chindasuinth" title="Chindasuinth">King Chindasuinth</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Code" title="Visigothic Code">Visigothic Code</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-google-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Eastern part the dominant state was the remaining <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>.
</p><p>In the feudal system, new princes and kings arose, the most powerful of which was arguably the Frankish ruler <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>. In 800, Charlemagne, reinforced by his massive territorial conquests, was crowned Emperor of the Romans (Imperator Romanorum) by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a>, effectively solidifying his power in western Europe. Charlemagne's reign marked the beginning of a new Germanic Roman Empire in the west, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. Outside his borders, new forces were gathering. The <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a> were marking out their territory, a <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Great Moravia</a> was growing, while the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> were securing their borders.
</p><p>For the duration of the 6th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> was embroiled in a series of deadly conflicts, first with the Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">Roman–Persian Wars</a>), followed by the onslaught of the arising Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a>). By 650, the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Palestine and Syria</a> were lost to the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_army" title="Rashidun army">Muslim forces</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Hispania</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">southern Italy</a> in the 7th and 8th centuries (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>). The Arab invasion from the east was stopped after the intervention of the <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian Empire</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">Han Tervel</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_Europe">Post-classical Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Post-classical Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Medieval demography</a></div>
<p>The Middle Ages are commonly dated from the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> (or by some scholars, before that) in the 5th century to the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> in the 16th century, marked by the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a>, the division of Western Christianity in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>, the rise of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, and the beginnings of European overseas expansion which allowed for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantium">Byzantium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Byzantium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3132" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/220px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/330px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/440px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> offering their fealty to the Virgin Mary inside the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many consider Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> (reigned 306–337) to be the first "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperor</a>". It was he who moved the imperial capital in 324 from <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, which re-founded as Constantinople, or Nova Roma ("<a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">New Rome</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The city of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> itself had not served as the capital since the reign of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> (284–305). Some date the beginnings of the Empire to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (379–395) and Christianity's official supplanting of the pagan <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman religion</a>, or following his death in 395, when the empire was split into two parts, with capitals in Rome and Constantinople. Others place it yet later in 476, when <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustulus" title="Romulus Augustulus">Romulus Augustulus</a>, traditionally considered the last western emperor, was deposed, thus leaving sole imperial authority with the emperor in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_East" title="Greek East">Greek East</a>. Others point to the reorganisation of the empire in the time of <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (c. 620) when Latin titles and usages were officially replaced with Greek versions. In any case, the changeover was gradual and by 330, when Constantine inaugurated his new capital, the process of hellenization and increasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianisation" title="Christianisation">Christianisation</a> was already under way. The Empire is generally considered to have ended after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> in 1453. The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> that afflicted the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, including its capital <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, in the years 541–542. It is estimated that the Plague of Justinian killed as many as 100 million people across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It caused <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Europe's population</a> to drop by around 50% between 541 and 700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> It also may have contributed to the success of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> During most of its existence, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> was one of the most powerful economic, cultural, and military forces in Europe, and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> was one of the largest and wealthiest cities in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_Conquest" title="Muslim Conquest">Muslim Conquest</a></div>
<p>The Early Middle Ages span roughly five centuries from 500 to 1000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_814.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="919" data-file-width="1399" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Europe_814.svg/300px-Europe_814.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Europe_814.svg/450px-Europe_814.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Europe_814.svg/600px-Europe_814.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_814.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the East and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast</a> of Europe new dominant states formed: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avar_Khaganate" title="Avar Khaganate">Avar Khaganate</a> (567–after 822), <a href="/wiki/Old_Great_Bulgaria" title="Old Great Bulgaria">Old Great Bulgaria</a> (632–668), the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazar Khaganate</a> (c. 650–969) and <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Danube Bulgaria</a> (founded by <a href="/wiki/Asparuh_of_Bulgaria" title="Asparuh of Bulgaria">Asparuh</a> in 680) were constantly rivaling the hegemony of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>.
</p><p>From the 7th century Byzantine history was greatly affected by the rise of Islam and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliphates" title="Caliphates">Caliphates</a>. Muslim <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> first invaded historically Roman territory under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr" title="Abū Bakr">Abū Bakr</a>, first Caliph of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, who entered <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Roman Syria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Mesopotamia" title="Roman Mesopotamia">Roman Mesopotamia</a>. As the Byzantines and neighboring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sasanids" title="Sasanids">Sasanids</a> were severely weakened by the time, amongst the most important reason(s) being the protracted, centuries-lasting and frequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_wars" title="Byzantine–Sasanian wars">Byzantine–Sasanian wars</a>, which included the climactic <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>, the second Caliph, the Muslims entirely toppled the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanid Persian Empire</a>, and decisively conquered Syria and Mesopotamia, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Palestine" title="Roman Palestine">Roman Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>, and parts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africa_Province" title="Africa Province">Roman North Africa</a>. In the mid 7th century AD, following the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a>, Islam penetrated into the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> region, of which parts <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_Wars" title="Russo-Persian Wars">would later</a> permanently become part of Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> This trend, which included the conquests by the invading Muslim forces and by that the spread of Islam as well continued under Umar's successors and under the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, which conquered the rest of Mediterranean North Africa and most of the <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">Iberian Peninsula</a>. Over the next centuries Muslim forces were able to take further European territory, including <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Cyprus in the Middle Ages">Cyprus</a>, Malta, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">Crete</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">Sicily and parts of southern Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Muslim conquest of Hispania began when the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berbers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arabs</a>) invaded the Christian <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigothic</a> kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Hispania</a> in the year 711, under the Berber general <a href="/wiki/Tariq_ibn_Ziyad" title="Tariq ibn Ziyad">Tariq ibn Ziyad</a>. They landed at <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> on 30 April and worked their way northward. Tariq's forces were joined the next year by those of his Arab superior, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musa_ibn_Nusair" title="Musa ibn Nusair">Musa ibn Nusair</a>. During the eight-year campaign most of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> was brought under Muslim rule – save for small areas in the northwest (<a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>) and largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basque_people" title="Basque people">Basque</a> regions in the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>. In 711, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigothic" title="Visigothic">Visigothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a> was very weakened because it was immersed in a serious internal crisis caused by a war of succession to the throne involving two Visigoth suitors. The Muslims took advantage of the crisis within the <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispano</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigothic" title="Visigothic">Visigothic</a> society to carry out their conquests. This territory, under the Arab name <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, became part of the expanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> empire.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">second siege of Constantinople</a> (717) ended unsuccessfully after the intervention of <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">Tervel of Bulgaria</a> and weakened the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> dynasty and reduced their prestige. In 722 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_Pelayo" title="Don Pelayo">Don Pelayo</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobleman" title="Nobleman">nobleman</a> of Visigothic origin, formed an army of 300 <a href="/wiki/Astures" title="Astures">Astur</a> soldiers, to confront Munuza's Muslim troops. In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Covadonga" title="Battle of Covadonga">battle of Covadonga</a>, the Astures defeated the Arab-Moors, who decided to retire. The Christian victory marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">Kingdom of Asturias</a>, whose first sovereign was Don Pelayo. The conquerors intended to continue their expansion in Europe and move northeast across the Pyrenees, but were defeated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Poitiers</a> in 732. The Umayyads were overthrown in 750 by the '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abb%C4%81sids" title="Abbāsids">Abbāsids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and, in 756, the Umayyads established an <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Emirate of Córdoba">independent emirate</a> in the Iberian Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Feudal_Christendom">Feudal Christendom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Feudal Christendom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_England" title="Medieval England">Medieval England</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Hungary" title="Medieval Hungary">Medieval Hungary</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Poland" title="Medieval Poland">Medieval Poland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="1932" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Europe_1000.jpg/300px-Europe_1000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Europe_1000.jpg/450px-Europe_1000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Europe_1000.jpg/600px-Europe_1000.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe in 1000, with most European states already formed</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1230_CE,_Europe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="438" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg/300px-1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg/450px-1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg/600px-1230_CE%2C_Europe.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1230_CE,_Europe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe around 1230, showing <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Mongol invasion of Kievan Rus'">Mongol incursions</a> in the east</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> emerged around 800, as <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, King of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> and part of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian dynasty</a>, was crowned by the pope as emperor. His empire based in modern France, the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> and Germany expanded into modern Hungary, Italy, <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, Lower Saxony and Spain. He and his father received substantial help from an alliance with the Pope, who wanted help against the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> His death marked the beginning of the end of the dynasty, which collapsed entirely by 888. The fragmentation of power led to semi-autonomy in the region, and has been defined as a critical starting point for the formation of <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a> in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the east, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> was established in 681 and became the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The powerful <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian Empire</a> was the main rival of Byzantium for control of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> for centuries and from the 9th century became the cultural centre of Slavic Europe. The <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Empire</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a> during the 9th century AD, at the <a href="/wiki/Preslav_Literary_School" title="Preslav Literary School">Preslav Literary School</a>, and experienced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_medieval_Bulgarian_culture" title="Golden Age of medieval Bulgarian culture">Golden Age</a> of Bulgarian cultural prosperity during the reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I the Great</a> (893–927). Two states, <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Great Moravia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, emerged among the Slavic peoples respectively in the 9th century. In the late 9th and 10th centuries, northern and western Europe felt the burgeoning power and influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> who raided, traded, conquered and settled swiftly and efficiently with their advanced seagoing vessels such as the <a href="/wiki/Longship" title="Longship">longships</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> had left a <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> influence on the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Invasion#Magyar_invasions_of_Europe" title="Invasion">Hungarians</a> pillaged mainland Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a> raided Bulgaria, Rus States and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> states. In the 10th century independent kingdoms were established in Central Europe including Poland and the newly settled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Croatia_(925%E2%80%931102)" title="Kingdom of Croatia (925–1102)">Kingdom of Croatia</a> also appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The subsequent period, ending around 1000, saw the further growth of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>, which weakened the Holy Roman Empire.
</p><p>In eastern Europe, <a href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgaria" title="Volga Bulgaria">Volga Bulgaria</a> became an Islamic state in 921, after <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alm%C4%B1%C5%9F_I&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Almış I (page does not exist)">Almış I</a> converted to Islam under the missionary efforts of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Fadlan" title="Ahmad ibn Fadlan">Ahmad ibn Fadlan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_medieval_Europe" title="Slavery in medieval Europe">Slavery in the early medieval period</a> had mostly died out in western Europe by about the year 1000 AD, replaced by <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>. It lingered longer in England and in peripheral areas linked to the Muslim world, where slavery continued to flourish. Church rules suppressed slavery of Christians. Most historians argue the transition was quite abrupt around 1000, but some see a gradual transition from about 300 to 1000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages">High Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First.Crusade.Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1402" data-file-width="1730" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First.Crusade.Map.jpg/220px-First.Crusade.Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First.Crusade.Map.jpg/330px-First.Crusade.Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First.Crusade.Map.jpg/440px-First.Crusade.Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First.Crusade.Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe in 1097, as the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> commences</div></div></div>
<p>The slumber of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Dark Ages</a> was shaken by a renewed crisis in the Church. In 1054, the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>, an insoluble split, occurred between the two remaining Christian seats in <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Constantinople</a> (modern Istanbul).
</p><p>The High Middle Ages of the 11th, 12th, and 13th centuries show a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">rapidly increasing population</a> of Europe, which caused great social and political change from the preceding era. By 1250, the robust population increase greatly benefited the economy, reaching levels it would not see again in some areas until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>From about the year 1000 onwards, Western Europe saw the last of the barbarian invasions and became more politically organized. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> had settled in Britain, Ireland, France and elsewhere, whilst Norse Christian kingdoms were developing in their Scandinavian homelands. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" title="Hungarian people">Magyars</a> had ceased their expansion in the 10th century, and by the year 1000, the Roman Catholic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_Kingdom" title="Apostolic Kingdom">Apostolic Kingdom</a> of Hungary was recognised in central Europe. With the brief exception of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Europe" title="Mongol invasion of Europe">Mongol invasions</a>, major barbarian incursions ceased.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Empire" title="Bulgarian Empire">Bulgarian</a> sovereignty was re-established with the <a href="/wiki/Uprising_of_Asen_and_Peter" title="Uprising of Asen and Peter">anti-Byzantine uprising of the Bulgarians and Vlachs</a> in 1185. The crusaders invaded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_empire" title="Byzantine empire">Byzantine empire</a>, captured Constantinople in 1204 and established their <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kaloyan_of_Bulgaria" title="Kaloyan of Bulgaria">Kaloyan of Bulgaria</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I,_Latin_Emperor" title="Baldwin I, Latin Emperor">Baldwin I</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Emperor_of_Constantinople" title="Latin Emperor of Constantinople">Latin Emperor of Constantinople</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople_(1205)" title="Battle of Adrianople (1205)">Battle of Adrianople</a> on 14 April 1205. The reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivan_Asen_II_of_Bulgaria" title="Ivan Asen II of Bulgaria">Ivan Asen II of Bulgaria</a> led to maximum territorial expansion and that of <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Alexander_of_Bulgaria" title="Ivan Alexander of Bulgaria">Ivan Alexander of Bulgaria</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Tarnovo_Artistic_School" title="Architecture of the Tarnovo Artistic School">Second Golden Age of Bulgarian culture</a>. The Byzantine Empire was fully re-established in 1261.
</p><p>In the 11th century, populations north of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> began to settle new lands, some of which had reverted to wilderness after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. In what is known as the "great clearances", vast forests and marshes of Europe were cleared and cultivated. At the same time settlements moved beyond the traditional boundaries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> to new frontiers in Europe, beyond the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a> river, tripling the size of Germany in the process. Crusaders founded <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">European colonies</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Iberian Peninsula was conquered</a> from the Muslims, and the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> colonised southern Italy, all part of the major population increase and resettlement pattern.
</p><p>The High Middle Ages produced many different forms of intellectual, spiritual and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">artistic works</a>. The most famous are the great cathedrals as expressions of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a>, which evolved from <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a>. This age saw the rise of modern nation-states in Western Europe and the ascent of the famous Italian <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>. The influential popes of the Catholic Church called volunteer armies from across Europe to a series of <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuq Turks</a>, who occupied the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>. The rediscovery of the works of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> led <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> and other thinkers to develop the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="A_divided_church">A divided church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: A divided church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a></div>
<p>The Great Schism between the Western (Catholic) and Eastern (Orthodox) Christian Churches was sparked in 1054 by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_IX" title="Pope Leo IX">Pope Leo IX</a> asserting authority over three of the seats in the <a href="/wiki/Pentarchy" title="Pentarchy">Pentarchy</a>, in Antioch, Jerusalem and Alexandria. Since the mid-8th century, the Byzantine Empire's borders had been shrinking in the face of Islamic expansion. <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> had been wrested back into Byzantine control by 1045, but the resurgent power of the Roman successors in the West claimed a right and a duty for the lost seats in Asia and Africa. Pope Leo sparked a further dispute by defending the <a href="/wiki/Filioque" title="Filioque">filioque</a> clause in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> which the West had adopted customarily. The Orthodox today state that the XXVIIIth Canon of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> explicitly proclaimed the equality of the Bishops of Rome and Constantinople. The Orthodox also state that the Bishop of Rome has authority only over his own diocese and does not have any authority outside his diocese. There were other less significant catalysts for the Schism however, including variance over <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>. The Schism of Roman Catholic and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> followed centuries of estrangement between the Latin and Greek worlds.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_wars">Holy wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Holy wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiegeofAntioch.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2849" data-file-width="3021" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SiegeofAntioch.jpeg/220px-SiegeofAntioch.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SiegeofAntioch.jpeg/330px-SiegeofAntioch.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SiegeofAntioch.jpeg/440px-SiegeofAntioch.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiegeofAntioch.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch" title="Siege of Antioch">Siege of Antioch</a>, from a medieval miniature painting, during the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a> was adopted by the newly created kingdoms of Central Europe: <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, Hungary and <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>. The Roman Catholic Church developed as a major power, leading to conflicts between the Pope and emperor. The geographic reach of the Roman Catholic Church expanded enormously due to the conversions of pagan kings (Scandinavia, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, Poland, Hungary), the Christian <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">crusades</a>. Most of Europe was Roman Catholic in the 15th century.
</p><p>Early signs of the rebirth of civilization in western Europe began to appear in the 11th century as trade started again in Italy, leading to the economic and cultural growth of independent <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Venice#History" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Florence" title="History of Florence">Florence</a>; at the same time, nation-states began to take form in places such as France, England, Spain, and Portugal, although the process of their formation (usually marked by rivalry between the monarchy, the aristocratic feudal lords and the church) actually took several centuries. These new nation-states began writing in their own cultural vernaculars, instead of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>. Notable figures of this movement would include <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christine_de_Pizan" title="Christine de Pizan">Christine de Pizan</a> (born Christina da Pizzano), the former writing in Italian, and the latter, although an Italian (Venice), relocated to France, writing in French. (See <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> for the latter two countries.) Elsewhere, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, essentially based in Germany and Italy, further fragmented into a myriad of feudal principalities or small city states, whose subjection to the emperor was only formal.
</p><p>The 14th century, when the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> came to power, is often called the <i>Age of the Mongols</i>. Mongol armies <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">expanded westward</a> under the command of <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu Khan</a>. Their western conquests included almost all of Russia (save <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novgorod" title="Novgorod">Novgorod</a>, which became a vassal),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kipchak-Cuman_Confederation" title="Kipchak-Cuman Confederation">Kipchak-Cuman Confederation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Second_Bulgarian_Empire" title="Second Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>, Hungary, and Poland managed to remain sovereign states. Mongolian records indicate that Batu Khan was planning a complete conquest of the remaining European powers, beginning with a winter attack on Austria, Italy and Germany, when he was recalled to <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> upon the death of Great Khan <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei</a>. Most historians believe only his death prevented the complete conquest of Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The areas of Eastern Europe and most of Central Asia that were under direct Mongol rule became known as the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>. Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uzbeg_Khan" title="Uzbeg Khan">Uzbeg Khan</a>, Islam became the official religion of the region in the early 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The invading Mongols, together with their mostly Turkic subjects, were known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatar_people" title="Tatar people">Tatars</a>. In Russia, the Tatars ruled the various states of the Rus' through vassalage for over 300 years.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG/220px-Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG/330px-Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG/440px-Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chrzest_Litwy_1387_Matejko.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Lithuania#Christianization_by_Jogaila_and_Vytautas" title="Christianization of Lithuania">Christianization of Lithuania</a> in 1387", oil on canvas by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko" title="Jan Matejko">Jan Matejko</a>, 1889, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Castle_in_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle in Warsaw">Royal Castle in Warsaw</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the Northern Europe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konrad_of_Masovia" title="Konrad of Masovia">Konrad of Masovia</a> gave <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno" title="Chełmno">Chełmno</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> in 1226 as a base for a Crusade against the <a href="/wiki/Old_Prussians" title="Old Prussians">Old Prussians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Brothers_of_the_Sword" title="Livonian Brothers of the Sword">Livonian Brothers of the Sword</a> were defeated by the Lithuanians, so in 1237 Gregory IX merged the remainder of the order into the Teutonic Order as the <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Order" title="Livonian Order">Livonian Order</a>. By the middle of the century, the Teutonic Knights completed their conquest of the Prussians before conquering and converting the Lithuanians in the subsequent decades. The order also came into conflict with the Eastern Orthodox Church of the <a href="/wiki/Pskov_Republic" title="Pskov Republic">Pskov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Novgorod_Republic" title="Novgorod Republic">Novgorod Republics</a>. In 1240 the Orthodox Novgorod army defeated the Catholic Swedes in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Neva" title="Battle of the Neva">Battle of the Neva</a>, and, two years later, they defeated the Livonian Order in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_on_the_Ice" title="Battle on the Ice">Battle on the Ice</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Krewo" title="Union of Krewo">Union of Krewo</a> in 1386, bringing two major changes in the history of the Grand Duchy of Lithuania: <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Lithuania" title="Christianization of Lithuania">conversion to Catholicism</a> and establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_union" title="Dynastic union">dynastic union</a> between the Grand Duchy of Lithuania and the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_the_Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Crown of the Kingdom of Poland">Crown of the Kingdom of Poland</a> marked both the greatest territorial expansion of the Grand Duchy and the defeat of the Teutonic Knights in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Grunwald" title="Battle of Grunwald">Battle of Grunwald</a> in 1410.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Ages">Late Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Late Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lex_mercatoria" title="Lex mercatoria">Lex mercatoria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Crisis of the Late Middle Ages">Crisis of the Late Middle Ages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Consulate_of_the_Sea" title="Consulate of the Sea">Consulate of the Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bubonic_plague-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bubonic_plague-en.svg/220px-Bubonic_plague-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bubonic_plague-en.svg/330px-Bubonic_plague-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bubonic_plague-en.svg/440px-Bubonic_plague-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bubonic_plague-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The spread of the "Black Death" from 1347 to 1351 through Europe</div></div></div>
<p>The Late Middle Ages spanned around the 14th and late 15th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Around 1300, centuries of European prosperity and growth came to a halt. A series of famines and plagues, such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" title="Great Famine of 1315–1317">Great Famine of 1315–1317</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, killed people in a matter of days, reducing the population of some areas by half as many survivors fled. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Kishlansky" title="Mark Kishlansky">Kishlansky</a> reports:
</p>
<dl><dd>The Black Death touched every aspect of life, hastening a process of social, economic, and cultural transformation already underway.... Fields were abandoned, workplaces stood idle, international trade was suspended. Traditional bonds of kinship, village, and even religion were broken amid the horrors of death, flight, and failed expectations. "People cared no more for dead men than we care for dead goats," wrote one survivor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Depopulation caused labor to become scarcer; the survivors were better paid and peasants could drop some of the burdens of feudalism. There was also social unrest; France and England experienced serious peasant risings including the <a href="/wiki/Jacquerie" title="Jacquerie">Jacquerie</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">Peasants' Revolt</a>. At the same time, the unity of the Catholic Church was shattered by the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Great Schism</a>. Collectively these events have been called the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Crisis of the Late Middle Ages">Crisis of the Late Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the 14th century, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> became one of the most important <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, an alliance of trading cities, facilitated the absorption of vast areas of Poland, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Terra_Mariana" title="Terra Mariana">Livonia</a> into trade with other European countries. This fed the growth of powerful states in this part of Europe including Poland–Lithuania, Hungary, Bohemia, and Muscovy later on. The conventional end of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> is usually associated with the fall of the city of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> in 1453. The Turks made the city the capital of their <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, which lasted until 1922 and included Egypt, Syria, and most of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a>, also sometimes referred to as the Turkish wars, marked an essential part of the history of the continent as a whole.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Bull_of_1356.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4672" data-file-width="4872" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Golden_Bull_of_1356.png/220px-Golden_Bull_of_1356.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Golden_Bull_of_1356.png/330px-Golden_Bull_of_1356.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Golden_Bull_of_1356.png/440px-Golden_Bull_of_1356.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Bull_of_1356.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> was a limited elective monarchy composed of hundreds of <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_in_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="List of states in the Holy Roman Empire">state-like entities</a></div></div></div>
<p>A key 15th-century development was the <a href="/wiki/Global_spread_of_the_printing_press" title="Global spread of the printing press">advent of the movable type of printing press</a> circa 1439 in Mainz,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-body_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-body-58">[58]</a></sup> building upon the impetus provided by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_paper" title="History of paper">prior introduction of paper</a> from China via the Arabs in the High Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-books-59">[59]</a></sup> Paper was already readily available in Europe by the late 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-books-59">[59]</a></sup> While forms of moveable type of printing press had been already used in China and Korea, the technique was singularly successful in Europe given the small number of characters of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, massively reducing costs of book production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The adoption of the technology across the continent at dazzling speed for the remaining part of the 15th century would usher a revolution and by 1500 over 200 cities in Europe had presses that printed between 8 and 20 million books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-body_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-body-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Homicide_rates_plunge_over_800_years">Homicide rates plunge over 800 years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Homicide rates plunge over 800 years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the local level, levels of violence were extremely high by modern standards in medieval and early modern Europe. They actually reached their peak during the late Middle Ages, having increased since the third century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Typically, small groups would battle their neighbors, using the farm tools at hand such as knives, sickles, hammers and axes. Mayhem and death were deliberate. The vast majority of people lived in rural areas. Cities were few, and small in size, but their concentration of population was conducive to violence. Long-term studies of places such as <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zurich</a> show the same trends as rural areas. Across Europe, <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> trends (not including military actions) show a steady long-term decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Regional differences were small, except that Italy's decline was later and slower. From approximately 1200 AD through 1800 AD, homicide rates from violent local episodes declined by a factor of ten, from approximately 32 deaths per 100 000 people to 3.2 per 100 000. In the 20th century the homicide rate fell to 1.4 per 100 000. Police forces seldom existed outside the cities; prisons only became common after 1800. Before then harsh penalties were imposed for homicide (severe whipping or execution) but they proved ineffective at controlling or reducing the insults to honor that precipitated most of the violence. The decline does not correlate with economics. Most historians attribute the trend in homicides to a steady increase in self-control of the sort promoted by Protestantism, and necessitated by schools and factories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Eisner" title="Manuel Eisner">Manuel Eisner</a> has summarized the patterns from over 300 historical studies.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Europe">Early modern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Early modern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">Early modern Europe</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648–1814">International relations, 1648–1814</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="943" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/220px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/330px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/440px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> (red) and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venetian</a> (green) maritime trade routes in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Early Modern period spans the centuries between the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, roughly from 1500 to 1800, or from the discovery of the New World in 1492 to the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> in 1789. The period is characterised by the rise in importance of science and increasingly rapid <a href="/wiki/History_of_technology" title="History of technology">technological progress</a>, secularised civic politics, and the nation state. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitalist_economy" title="Capitalist economy">Capitalist economies</a> began their rise, and the early modern period also saw the rise and dominance of the economic theory of <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a>. As such, the early modern period represents the decline and eventual disappearance, in much of the European sphere, of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>, serfdom and the power of the Catholic Church. The period includes the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, the disastrous <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonisation_of_the_Americas" title="European colonisation of the Americas">European colonisation of the Americas</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_witch-hunts" title="European witch-hunts">European witch-hunts</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacioli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/250px-Pacioli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/375px-Pacioli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pacioli.jpg/500px-Pacioli.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacioli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_Luca_Pacioli" title="Portrait of Luca Pacioli">Portrait of Luca Pacioli</a></i>, the founder of accounting, by <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_de%27_Barbari" title="Jacopo de' Barbari">Jacopo de' Barbari</a> (<a href="/wiki/Museo_di_Capodimonte" title="Museo di Capodimonte">Museo di Capodimonte</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>Despite these crises, the 14th century was also a time of great progress within the arts and sciences. A renewed interest in ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>.
</p><p>The Renaissance was a cultural movement that profoundly affected European intellectual life in the early modern period. Beginning in Italy, and spreading to the north, west and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Europe" title="Middle Europe">middle Europe</a> during a cultural lag of some two and a half centuries, its influence affected literature, philosophy, art, politics, science, history, religion, and other aspects of intellectual inquiry.
</p><p>The Italian <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> (Francesco Petrarca), deemed the first full-blooded Humanist, wrote in the 1330s: "I am alive now, yet I would rather have been born in another time." He was enthusiastic about Greek and Roman antiquity. In the 15th and 16th centuries the continuing enthusiasm for the ancients was reinforced by the feeling that the inherited culture was dissolving and here was a storehouse of ideas and attitudes with which to rebuild. <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Palmieri" title="Matteo Palmieri">Matteo Palmieri</a> wrote in the 1430s: "Now indeed may every thoughtful spirit thank god that it has been permitted to him to be born in a new age." The renaissance was born: a new age where learning was very important.
</p><p>The Renaissance was inspired by the growth in the study of Latin and Greek texts and the admiration of the Greco-Roman era as a golden age. This prompted many artists and writers to begin drawing from Roman and Greek examples for their works, but there was also much innovation in this period, especially by multi-faceted artists such as <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>. The Humanists saw their repossession of a great past as a Renaissance – a rebirth of civilization itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important political precedents were also set in this period. <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a>'s political writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince" title="The Prince">The Prince</a></i> influenced later absolutism and realpolitik. Also important were the many patrons who ruled states and used the artistry of the Renaissance as a sign of their power.
</p><p>In all, the Renaissance could be viewed as an attempt by intellectuals to study and improve the secular and worldly, both through the revival of ideas from antiquity and through novel approaches to thought – the immediate past being too "Gothic" in language, thought and sensibility.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_and_trade">Exploration and trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Exploration and trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cantino_planisphere_(1502).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4998" data-file-width="10643" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/220px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/330px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/440px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cantino_planisphere_(1502).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cantino_planisphere" title="Cantino planisphere">Cantino planisphere</a>, 1502, earliest chart showing explorations by <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Columbus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pedro_%C3%81lvares_Cabral" title="Pedro Álvares Cabral">Cabral</a></div></div></div>
<p>Toward the end of the period, an era of discovery began. The growth of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453, cut off trading possibilities with the east. Western Europe was forced to discover new trading routes, as happened with Columbus' travel to the Americas in 1492, and <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>'s circumnavigation of India and Africa in 1498.
</p><p>The numerous wars did not prevent European states from exploring and conquering wide portions of the world, from Africa to Asia and the newly discovered Americas.
