In the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> led the way in geographical exploration along the coast of Africa in search of a maritime route to India, followed by Spain near the close of the 15th century, dividing their exploration of the world according to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> in 1494.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They were the first states to set up colonies in America and European <a href="/wiki/Factory_(trading_post)" title="Factory (trading post)">trading posts (factories)</a> along the shores of Africa and Asia, establishing the first direct European diplomatic contacts with Southeast Asian states in 1511, China in 1513 and Japan in 1542. In 1552, Russian tsar <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan the Terrible</a> conquered two major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatar" title="Tatar">Tatar</a> khanates, the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Kazan" title="Khanate of Kazan">Khanate of Kazan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan_Khanate" title="Astrakhan Khanate">Astrakhan Khanate</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Yermak_Timofeyevich" title="Yermak Timofeyevich">Yermak</a>'s voyage of 1580 led to the annexation of the Tatar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siberian_Khanate" title="Siberian Khanate">Siberian Khanate</a> into Russia, and the Russians would soon after conquer the rest of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, steadily expanding to the east and south over the next centuries. Oceanic explorations soon followed by France, England and the Netherlands, who explored the Portuguese and Spanish trade routes into the Pacific Ocean, reaching Australia in 1606<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and New Zealand in 1642.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation">Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:334px;max-width:334px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/186px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/279px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/372px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg 2x" width="186"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_95_Thesen.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="4611" data-file-width="3228" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/140px-Luther_95_Thesen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/210px-Luther_95_Thesen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/280px-Luther_95_Thesen.png 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Martin Luther initiated the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a> with his <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i> in 1517</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1654" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg/220px-Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg/330px-Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg/440px-Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Habsburg_Map_1547.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a> realms (green) under <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor</a></div></div></div>
<p>With the development of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, new ideas spread throughout Europe and challenged traditional doctrines in science and theology. Simultaneously, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> under German <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> questioned Papal authority. The most common dating of the Reformation begins in 1517, when Luther published <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ninety-Five_Theses" title="The Ninety-Five Theses">The Ninety-Five Theses</a></i>, and concludes in 1648 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Westphalia" title="Treaty of Westphalia">Treaty of Westphalia</a> that ended years of <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">European religious wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this period corruption in the Catholic Church led to a sharp backlash in the Protestant Reformation. It gained many followers especially among princes and kings seeking a stronger state by ending the influence of the Catholic Church. Figures other than <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> began to emerge as well like <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> had influence in many countries and King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> who broke away from the Catholic Church in England and set up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a>; his daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth</a> finished the organization of the church. These religious divisions brought on a wave of wars inspired and driven by religion but also by the ambitious monarchs in Western Europe who were becoming more centralized and powerful.
</p><p>The Protestant Reformation also led to a strong reform movement in the Catholic Church called the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>, which aimed to reduce corruption as well as to improve and strengthen Catholic dogma. Two important groups in the Catholic Church who emerged from this movement were the <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>, who helped keep Spain, Portugal, Poland, and other European countries within the Catholic fold, and the Oratorians of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Philip_Neri" title="Saint Philip Neri">Saint Philip Neri</a>, who ministered to the faithful in Rome, restoring their confidence in the Church of Jesus Christ that subsisted substantially in the Church of Rome. Still, the Catholic Church was somewhat weakened by the Reformation, portions of Europe were no longer under its sway and kings in the remaining Catholic countries began to take control of the church institutions within their kingdoms.
</p><p>Unlike many European countries, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> and Hungary were more tolerant. While still enforcing the predominance of Catholicism, they continued to allow the large religious minorities to maintain their faiths, traditions and customs. The Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth became divided among Catholics, Protestants, Orthodox, Jews and a small Muslim population.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1450" data-file-width="924" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg/170px-Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg/255px-Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg/340px-Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_As_A_Queen_Sebastian_Munster_1570.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Europa_regina" title="Europa regina">Europa regina</a>, 1570 print by <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_M%C3%BCnster" title="Sebastian Münster">Sebastian Münster</a> of <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a></div></div></div>
<p>Another development was the idea of 'European superiority'. The ideal of civilization was taken over from the ancient Greeks and Romans: Discipline, education and living in the city were required to make people civilized; Europeans and non-Europeans were judged for their civility, and Europe regarded itself as superior to other continents. There was a movement by some such as <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Montaigne</a> that regarded the non-Europeans as a better, more natural and primitive people. Post services were founded all over Europe, which allowed a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanistic</a> interconnected network of intellectuals across Europe, despite religious divisions. However, the Roman Catholic Church banned many leading scientific works; this led to an intellectual advantage for Protestant countries, where the banning of books was regionally organised. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> and other advocates of science tried to create unity in Europe by focusing on the unity in nature.<sup><a href="#Notes">1</a></sup> In the 15th century, at the end of the Middle Ages, powerful sovereign states were appearing, built by the <a href="/wiki/New_Monarchs" title="New Monarchs">New Monarchs</a> who were centralising power in France, England, and Spain. On the other hand, the Parliament in the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> grew in power, taking legislative rights from the Polish king. The new state power was contested by parliaments in other countries especially England. New kinds of states emerged which were co-operation agreements among territorial rulers, cities, farmer republics and knights.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="368" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/170px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/255px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/340px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a>, the Father of international law.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercantilism_and_colonial_expansion">Mercantilism and colonial expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mercantilism and colonial expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Mercantilism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonisation2.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="810" data-file-width="1849" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Colonisation2.gif/220px-Colonisation2.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Colonisation2.gif/330px-Colonisation2.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Colonisation2.gif/440px-Colonisation2.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonisation2.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animated map showing the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Colonial_empire" title="Colonial empire">Colonial empires</a> from 1492 to the present</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian</a> states (Spain and Portugal) were able to dominate colonial activity in the 16th century. The Portuguese forged the first global empire in the 15th and 16th century, whilst during the 16th century and the first half of the 17th century, the Spanish under the crown of Castile became the most powerful global empire in the world. Spanish dominance in America was increasingly challenged by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_colonisation_of_the_Americas" title="British colonisation of the Americas">British</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_colonisation_of_the_Americas" title="French colonisation of the Americas">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Dutch colonization of the Americas">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">Swedish</a> colonial efforts of the 17th and 18th centuries. New forms of trade and expanding horizons made new forms of <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">law</a> and economics necessary.
</p><p>Colonial expansion continued in the following centuries (with some setbacks, such as successful wars of independence in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">British American colonies</a> and then later <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentine_War_of_Independence" title="Argentine War of Independence">Argentina</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_Independence" title="Brazilian Independence">Brazil</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American_wars_of_independence" title="Latin American wars of independence">others</a> amid European turmoil of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>). Spain had control of a large part of North America, all of Central America and a great part of South America, the Caribbean and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>; Britain took the whole of Australia and New Zealand, most of India, and large parts of Africa and North America; France held parts of Canada and India (nearly all of which was lost to Britain <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">in 1763</a>), <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">Indochina</a>, large parts of Africa and the Caribbean islands; the Netherlands gained the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">East Indies</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>) and islands in the Caribbean; Portugal obtained Brazil and several territories in Africa and Asia; and later, powers such as Germany, Belgium, Italy and Russia acquired further colonies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>This expansion helped the economy of the countries owning them. <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Trade</a> flourished, because of the minor stability of the empires. By the late 16th century, American silver accounted for one-fifth of Spain's total budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colony</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> was one of richest European colonies in the 18th century, operating on a <a href="/wiki/Plantation_economy" title="Plantation economy">plantation economy</a> fueled by <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Haiti" title="Slavery in Haiti">slave labor</a>. During the period of French rule, <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> produced in Saint-Domingue comprised thirty percent of total French trade while its sugar exports represented forty percent of the Atlantic market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_of_the_17th_century">Crisis of the 17th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Crisis of the 17th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg/220px-Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg/330px-Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg/440px-Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Defenestration-prague-1618.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary woodcut depicting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Defenestration_of_Prague" title="Second Defenestration of Prague">Second Defenestration of Prague</a> (1618), which marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Revolt" title="Bohemian Revolt">Bohemian Revolt</a>, which began the first part of the Thirty Years' War.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_General_Crisis" title="The General Crisis">The General Crisis</a></div>
<p>The 17th century was an era of crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Parker_and_Lesley_M._Smith,_eds._1997_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Parker_and_Lesley_M._Smith,_eds._1997-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trevor_Aston_1965_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trevor_Aston_1965-77">[77]</a></sup> Many historians have rejected the idea, while others promote it as an invaluable insight into the warfare, politics, economics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and even art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> (1618–1648) focused attention on the massive horrors that wars could bring to entire populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The 1640s in particular saw more state breakdowns around the world than any previous or subsequent period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Parker_and_Lesley_M._Smith,_eds._1997_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Parker_and_Lesley_M._Smith,_eds._1997-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trevor_Aston_1965_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trevor_Aston_1965-77">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, the largest state in Europe, temporarily disappeared. In addition, there were secessions and upheavals in several parts of the Spanish empire, the world's first global empire. In Britain the entire <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">Stuart monarchy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">North American colonies</a>) rebelled. Political insurgency and a spate of popular revolts seldom equalled shook the foundations of most states in Europe and Asia. More wars took place around the world in the mid-17th century than in almost any other period of recorded history. The crises spread far beyond Europe – for example <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming China</a>, the most populous state in the world, collapsed. Across the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, the mid-17th century experienced almost unprecedented death rates. Geoffrey Parker, a British historian, suggests that environmental factors may have been in part to blame, especially global cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_absolutism">Age of absolutism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Age of absolutism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_absolutism" title="Political absolutism">Political absolutism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648–1814">International relations, 1648–1814</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg/220px-M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg/330px-M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg/440px-M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A1ria_Ter%C3%A9zia_koron%C3%A1z%C3%A1sa_a_Szent_M%C3%A1rton_sz%C3%A9kesegyh%C3%A1zban.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maria Theresa being crowned Queen of Hungary in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Martin%27s_Cathedral,_Bratislava" title="St. Martin's Cathedral, Bratislava">St. Martin's Cathedral</a>, Pressburg (<a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The "absolute" rule of powerful monarchs such as <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> (ruled France 1643–1715),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> (ruled Russia 1682–1725),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maria_Theresa" title="Maria Theresa">Maria Theresa</a> (ruled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburg_lands" title="Habsburg lands">Habsburg lands</a> 1740–1780) and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a> (ruled Prussia 1740–86),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> produced powerful centralized states, with strong armies and powerful bureaucracies, all under the control of the king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the early part of this period, capitalism (through mercantilism) was replacing feudalism as the principal form of economic organisation, at least in the western half of Europe. The expanding colonial frontiers resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Revolution" title="Commercial Revolution">Commercial Revolution</a>. The period is noted for the rise of modern science and the application of its findings to technological improvements, which animated the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> after 1750.
</p><p>The Reformation had profound effects on the unity of Europe. Not only were nations divided one from another by their religious orientation, but some states were torn apart internally by religious strife, avidly fostered by their external enemies. France suffered this fate in the 16th century in the series of conflicts known as the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">French Wars of Religion</a>, which ended in the triumph of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Bourbon" title="House of Bourbon">Bourbon Dynasty</a>. England avoided this fate for a while and settled down under <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a> to a moderate <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>. Much of modern-day Germany was made up of numerous small sovereign states under the theoretical framework of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, which was further divided along internally drawn sectarian lines. The <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> is notable in this time for its <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious indifference</a> and a general immunity to the horrors of European religious strife.
</p>
<h4><span id="Thirty_Years.27_War_1618.E2.80.931648"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thirty_Years'_War_1618–1648">Thirty Years' War 1618–1648</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Thirty Years' War 1618–1648">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> was fought between 1618 and 1648, across Germany and neighbouring areas, and involved most of the major European powers except England and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Beginning as a religious conflict between Protestants and Catholics in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, it quickly developed into a general war involving Catholics versus Protestants for the most part. The major impact of the war, in which mercenary armies were extensively used, was the devastation of entire regions scavenged bare by the foraging armies. Episodes of widespread famine and disease, and the breakup of family life, devastated the population of the German states and, to a lesser extent, the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Bohemia" title="Crown of Bohemia">Crown of Bohemia</a> and northern parts of Italy, while bankrupting many of the regional powers involved. Between one-fourth and one-third of the German population perished from direct military causes or from disease and starvation, as well as postponed births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_1648.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1367" data-file-width="1894" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/220px-Europe_map_1648.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/330px-Europe_map_1648.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Europe_map_1648.PNG/440px-Europe_map_1648.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_1648.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe after the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> in 1648</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>, which ended the war in favour of nations deciding their own religious allegiance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_absolutism" title="Political absolutism">absolutism</a> became the norm of the continent, while parts of Europe experimented with constitutions foreshadowed by the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> and particularly the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>. European military conflict did not cease, but had less disruptive effects on the lives of Europeans. In the advanced northwest, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" title="The Enlightenment">the Enlightenment</a> gave a philosophical underpinning to the new outlook, and the continued spread of literacy, made possible by the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, created new secular forces in thought.
</p><p>From the Union of Krewo (see above) central and eastern Europe was dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>. In the 16th and 17th centuries Central and Eastern Europe was an arena of conflict for domination of the continent between <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Sweden</a>, the Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth (involved in series of wars, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khmelnytsky_uprising" title="Khmelnytsky uprising">Khmelnytsky uprising</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Polish_War_(1654%E2%80%9367)" title="Russo-Polish War (1654–67)">Russo-Polish War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deluge_(history)" title="Deluge (history)">Deluge</a>, etc.) and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. This period saw a gradual decline of these three powers which were eventually replaced by new enlightened absolutist monarchies: Russia, Prussia and Austria (the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_monarchy" title="Habsburg monarchy">Habsburg monarchy</a>). By the turn of the 19th century they had become new powers, having <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">divided Poland</a> between themselves, with Sweden and Turkey having experienced substantial territorial losses to Russia and Austria respectively as well as pauperisation.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1842" data-file-width="2783" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg/220px-Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg/330px-Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg/440px-Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vienna_Battle_1683.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vienna" title="Battle of Vienna">Battle of Vienna</a> in 1683 marked the historic end of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman expansion into Europe</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_the_Spanish_Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: War of the Spanish Succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a> (1701–1715) was a major war with France opposed by a coalition of England, the Netherlands, the Habsburg monarchy, and Prussia. <a href="/wiki/John_Churchill,_1st_Duke_of_Marlborough" title="John Churchill, 1st Duke of Marlborough">Duke of Marlborough</a> commanded the English and Dutch victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Blenheim" title="Battle of Blenheim">Battle of Blenheim</a> in 1704. The main issue was whether France under King Louis XIV would take control of Spain's very extensive possessions and thereby become by far the dominant power, or be forced to share power with other major nations. After initial allied successes, the long war produced a military stalemate and ended with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Utrecht" title="Treaty of Utrecht">Treaty of Utrecht</a>, which was based on a balance of power in Europe. Historian <a href="/wiki/Russell_Weigley" title="Russell Weigley">Russell Weigley</a> argues that the many wars almost never accomplished more than they cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> British historian <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">G. M. Trevelyan</a> argues:
</p>
<dl><dd>That Treaty [of Utrecht], which ushered in the stable and characteristic period of Eighteenth-Century civilization, marked the end of danger to Europe from the old French monarchy, and it marked a change of no less significance to the world at large – the maritime, commercial and financial supremacy of Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prussia">Prussia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Prussia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, king of Prussia 1740–86, modernized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prussian_army" title="Prussian army">Prussian army</a>, introduced new tactical and strategic concepts, fought mostly successful wars (<a href="/wiki/Silesian_Wars" title="Silesian Wars">Silesian Wars</a>, Seven Years' War) and doubled the size of Prussia. Frederick had a rationale based on Enlightenment thought: he fought total wars for limited objectives. The goal was to convince rival kings that it was better to negotiate and make peace than to fight him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russia_1533-1896.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3420" data-file-width="4820" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Russia_1533-1896.gif/250px-Russia_1533-1896.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Russia_1533-1896.gif/375px-Russia_1533-1896.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Russia_1533-1896.gif/500px-Russia_1533-1896.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russia_1533-1896.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian expansion in Eurasia between 1533 and 1894</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Territorial_evolution_of_Russia" title="Territorial evolution of Russia">Territorial evolution of Russia</a></div>
<p>Russia fought numerous wars to achieve rapid expansion toward the east – i.e. <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Far East</a>, south, to the Black Sea, and south-east and to central Asia. Russia boasted a <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">large and powerful army</a>, a very large and complex internal bureaucracy, and a splendid court that rivaled Paris and London. However the government was living far beyond its means and seized <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Church</a> lands, leaving organized religion in a weak condition. Throughout the 18th century Russia remained "a poor, backward, overwhelmingly agricultural, and illiterate country."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlightenment">Enlightenment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Enlightenment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a></div>
<p>The <i>Enlightenment</i> was a powerful, widespread cultural movement of intellectuals beginning in late 17th-century Europe emphasizing the power of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> rather than tradition; it was especially favourable to science (especially Isaac Newton's physics) and hostile to religious orthodoxy (especially of the Catholic Church).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> It sought to analyze and reform society using reason, to challenge ideas grounded in tradition and faith, and to advance knowledge through the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>. It promoted scientific thought, skepticism, and intellectual interchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Charles_Kors_2003_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Charles_Kors_2003-95">[95]</a></sup> The Enlightenment was a revolution in human thought. This new way of thinking was that rational thought begins with clearly stated principles, uses correct logic to arrive at conclusions, tests the conclusions against evidence, and then revises the principles in light of the evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Charles_Kors_2003_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Charles_Kors_2003-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:288px;max-width:288px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1916" data-file-width="1364" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/140px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/210px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/280px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_(painted_portrait).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="1448" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/140px-Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/210px-Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg/280px-Jean-Jacques_Rousseau_%28painted_portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Enlightenment thinkers opposed superstition. Some Enlightenment thinkers collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">Enlightened despots</a>, absolutist rulers who attempted to forcibly impose some of the new ideas about government into practice. The ideas of the Enlightenment exerted significant influence on the culture, politics, and governments of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Originating in the 17th century, it was sparked by philosophers <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> (1562–1626), <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> (1632–1677), <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> (1632–1704), <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Bayle" title="Pierre Bayle">Pierre Bayle</a> (1647–1706), <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> (1694–1778), <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Francis Hutcheson</a> (1694–1746), <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> (1711–1776) and physicist <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1643–1727).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Ruling princes often endorsed and fostered these figures and even attempted to apply their ideas of government in what was known as <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">enlightened absolutism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a> is closely tied to the Enlightenment, as its discoveries overturned many traditional concepts and introduced new perspectives on nature and man's place within it. The Enlightenment flourished until about 1790–1800, at which point the Enlightenment, with its emphasis on reason, gave way to <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>, which placed a new emphasis on emotion; a <a href="/wiki/Counter-Enlightenment" title="Counter-Enlightenment">Counter-Enlightenment</a> began to increase in prominence. The Romantics argued that the Enlightenment was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reductionistic" title="Reductionistic">reductionistic</a> insofar as it had largely ignored the forces of imagination, mystery, and sentiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casey_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casey-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In France, Enlightenment was based in the <a href="/wiki/Salon_(gathering)" title="Salon (gathering)">salons</a> and culminated in the great <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A9die" title="Encyclopédie">Encyclopédie</a></i> (1751–72) edited by <a href="/wiki/Denis_Diderot" title="Denis Diderot">Denis Diderot</a> (1713–1784) and (until 1759) <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d'Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> (1717–1783) with contributions by hundreds of leading intellectuals who were called <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">philosophes</a></i>, notably <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> (1694–1778), <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Rousseau</a> (1712–1778) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_de_Secondat,_Baron_de_Montesquieu" title="Charles de Secondat, Baron de Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> (1689–1755). Some 25,000 copies of the 35 volume encyclopedia were sold, half of them outside France. These new intellectual strains would spread to urban centres across Europe, notably England, Scotland, the German states, the Netherlands, Poland, Russia, Italy, Austria, and Spain, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirteen_colonies" title="Thirteen colonies">Britain's American colonies.</a>
</p><p>The political ideals of the Enlightenment influenced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Declaration_of_Independence" title="American Declaration of Independence">American Declaration of Independence</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">United States Bill of Rights</a>, the French <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>, and the Polish–Lithuanian <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_3_May_1791" title="Constitution of 3 May 1791">Constitution of 3 May 1791</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taking a long-term historical perspective, Norman Davies has argued that <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a> was a powerful force on behalf of Liberalism and Enlightenment ideas in Europe, from about 1700 to the 20th century. It expanded rapidly during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, reaching practically every country in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Prominent members included <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Sir Robert Walpole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. Steven C. Bullock notes that in the late 18th century, English lodges were headed by the Prince of Wales, Prussian lodges by king <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, and French lodges by royal princes. Emperor Napoleon selected as Grand Master of France his own brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The great enemy of Freemasonry was the Roman Catholic Church, so that in countries with a large Catholic element, such as France, Italy, Austria, Spain and Mexico, much of the ferocity of the political battles involve the confrontation between supporters of the Church versus active Masons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> 20th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totalitarian" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> and revolutionary movements, especially the <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">Fascists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communists</a>, crushed the Freemasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Modern_Europe"></span>
</p>
<h2><span id="From_revolution_to_imperialism_.281789.E2.80.931914.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="From_revolution_to_imperialism_(1789–1914)">From revolution to imperialism (1789–1914)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: From revolution to imperialism (1789–1914)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Revolution" title="Age of Revolution">Age of Revolution</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1815_map_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1503" data-file-width="2284" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/450px-Europe_1815_map_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/675px-Europe_1815_map_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/900px-Europe_1815_map_en.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1815_map_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The boundaries set by the Congress of Vienna, 1815.</div></div></div>
<p>The "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_long_19th_century" title="The long 19th century">long 19th century</a>", from 1789 to 1914 saw the drastic social, political and economic changes initiated by the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>. Following the reorganisation of the political map of Europe at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> in 1815, Europe experienced the rise of Nationalism, the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> and the peak of the British Empire, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Decline of the Ottoman Empire">decline of the Ottoman Empire</a>. Finally, the rise of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a> initiated the course of events that culminated in the outbreak of the First World War in 1914.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_Revolution">Industrial Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Industrial Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dore_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dore_London.jpg/220px-Dore_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dore_London.jpg/330px-Dore_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dore_London.jpg/440px-Dore_London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dore_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>London's chimney sky in 1870, by <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">Gustave Doré</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Industrial Revolution was a period in the late 18th century and early 19th century when major changes in agriculture, manufacturing, and transport impacted Britain and subsequently spread to the United States and Western Europe, a process that continues as industrialisation. Technological advancements, most notably the utilization of the steam engine, were major catalysts in the industrialisation process. It started in England and Scotland in the mid-18th century with the mechanisation of the textile industries, the development of iron-making techniques and the increased use of coal as the main fuel. Trade expansion was enabled by the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>, improved roads and railways. The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam power</a> (fuelled primarily by coal) and powered machinery (mainly in <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>) underpinned the dramatic increases in production capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The development of all-metal <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a> in the first two decades of the 19th century facilitated the manufacture of more production machines for manufacturing in other industries. The effects spread throughout Western Europe and North America during the 19th century, eventually affecting most of the world. The impact of this change on society was enormous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Era_of_the_French_Revolution">Era of the French Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Era of the French Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Revolutions" title="Atlantic Revolutions">Atlantic Revolutions</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_and_Napoleonic_Wars" title="French Revolutionary and Napoleonic Wars">French Revolutionary and Napoleonic Wars</a></div>
<p>Historians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R.R._Palmer" title="R.R. Palmer">R.R. Palmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joel_Colton" title="Joel Colton">Joel Colton</a> argue:
</p>
<dl><dd>In 1789 France fell into revolution, and the world has never since been the same. The French Revolution was by far the most momentous upheaval of the whole revolutionary age. It replaced the "old regime" with "modern society," and at its extreme phase became very radical, so much so that all later revolutionary movements have looked back to it as a predecessor to themselves.... From the 1760s to 1848, the role of France was decisive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The era of the French Revolution and the subsequent Napoleonic wars was a difficult time for monarchs. Tsar <a href="/wiki/Paul_I_of_Russia" title="Paul I of Russia">Paul I of Russia</a> was assassinated; King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI of France</a> was executed, as was his queen <a href="/wiki/Marie_Antoinette" title="Marie Antoinette">Marie Antoinette</a>. Furthermore, kings <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV_of_Spain" title="Charles IV of Spain">Charles IV of Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_VII_of_Spain" title="Ferdinand VII of Spain">Ferdinand VII of Spain</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gustav_IV_Adolf_of_Sweden" title="Gustav IV Adolf of Sweden">Gustav IV Adolf of Sweden</a> were deposed as were ultimately the Emperor Napoleon and all of the relatives he had installed on various European thrones. King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William III of Prussia">Frederick William III of Prussia</a> and Emperor <a href="/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor">Francis II of Austria</a> barely clung to their thrones. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III of Great Britain</a> lost the better part of the First British Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> (1775–1783) was the first successful revolt of a colony against a European power. It proclaimed, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, that "all men are created equal," a position based on the principles of the Enlightenment. It rejected aristocracy and established a <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican form of government</a> under <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> that attracted worldwide attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The French Revolution (1789–1804) was a product of the same democratic forces in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_World" title="Atlantic World">Atlantic World</a> and had an even greater impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Victor_Alphonse_Aulard" title="François Victor Alphonse Aulard">French historian François Aulard</a> says:
</p>
<dl><dd>From the social point of view, the Revolution consisted in the suppression of what was called the feudal system, in the emancipation of the individual, in greater division of landed property, the abolition of the privileges of noble birth, the establishment of equality, the simplification of life.... The French Revolution differed from other revolutions in being not merely national, for it aimed at benefiting all humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5877" data-file-width="7877" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/220px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/330px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/440px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The storming of the <a href="/wiki/Bastille" title="Bastille">Bastille</a> in the French Revolution of 1789</div></div></div>
<p>French intervention in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> had nearly bankrupted the state. After repeated failed attempts at financial reform, King Louis XVI had to convene the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estates-General_of_1789" title="Estates-General of 1789">Estates-General</a>, a representative body of the country made up of three estates: the clergy, the nobility, and the commoners. The third estate, joined by members of the other two, declared itself to be a <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(French_Revolution)" title="National Assembly (French Revolution)">National Assembly</a> and swore an <a href="/wiki/Tennis_Court_Oath" title="Tennis Court Oath">oath</a> not to dissolve until France had a constitution and created, in July, the <a href="/wiki/National_Constituent_Assembly_(France)" title="National Constituent Assembly (France)">National Constituent Assembly</a>. At the same time the people of Paris revolted, famously <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">storming the Bastille prison on 14 July 1789</a>.
</p><p>At the time the assembly wanted to create a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, and over the following two years passed various laws including the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_feudalism_in_France" title="Abolition of feudalism in France">abolition of feudalism</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Constitution_of_the_Clergy" title="Civil Constitution of the Clergy">fundamental change</a> in the relationship between France and Rome. At first the king agreed with these changes and enjoyed reasonable popularity with the people. As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-royalism" title="Anti-royalism">anti-royalism</a> increased along with threat of foreign invasion, the king tried to flee and join France's enemies. He was captured and on 21 January 1793, having been convicted of treason, he was guillotined.
</p><p>On 20 September 1792 the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> abolished the monarchy and declared France a republic. Due to the emergency of <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">war</a>, the National Convention created the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Public_Safety" title="Committee of Public Safety">Committee of Public Safety</a>, controlled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximilien_de_Robespierre" title="Maximilien de Robespierre">Maximilien de Robespierre</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacobin_Club" title="Jacobin Club">Jacobin Club</a>, to act as the country's executive. Under Robespierre, the committee initiated the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>, during which up to 40,000 people were executed in Paris, mainly nobles and those convicted by the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Tribunal" title="Revolutionary Tribunal">Revolutionary Tribunal</a>, often on the flimsiest of evidence. Internal tensions at Paris drove the Committee towards increasing assertions of radicalism and increasing suspicions, fueling new terror: A few months into this phase, more and more prominent revolutionaries were being sent to the guillotine by Robespierre and his faction, for example <a href="/wiki/Madame_Roland" title="Madame Roland">Madame Roland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georges_Danton" title="Georges Danton">Georges Danton</a>. Elsewhere in the country, counter-revolutionary <a href="/wiki/War_in_the_Vend%C3%A9e" title="War in the Vendée">insurrections</a> were brutally suppressed. The regime was overthrown in the <a href="/wiki/Thermidorian_Reaction" title="Thermidorian Reaction">coup of 9 Thermidor</a> (27 July 1794) and Robespierre was executed. The regime which followed ended the Terror and relaxed Robespierre's more extreme policies.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleon">Napoleon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Napoleon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> was one of the world's most famous soldiers and statesmen, leading France to great victories over numerous European enemies. Despite modest origins he became <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor</a> and restructured much of European diplomacy, politics and law, until he was forced to abdicate in 1814. His 100-day comeback in 1815 failed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a>, and he died in exile on a remote island, remembered as a great hero by many Frenchmen and as a great villain by British and other enemies.
</p><p>Napoleon, despite his youth, was France's most successful general in the Revolutionary wars, having conquered large parts of Italy and forced the Austrians to sue for peace. In 1799 on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/18_Brumaire" title="18 Brumaire">18 Brumaire</a> (9 November) he overthrew the feeble government, replacing it with the <a href="/wiki/French_Consulate" title="French Consulate">Consulate</a>, which he dominated. He gained popularity in France by restoring the Church, keeping taxes low, centralizing power in Paris, and winning glory on the battlefield. In 1804 he crowned himself <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">Emperor</a>. In 1805, Napoleon planned to invade Britain, but a renewed British alliance with Russia and Austria (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Coalition" title="Third Coalition">Third Coalition</a>), forced him to turn his attention towards the continent, while at the same time the French fleet was demolished by the British at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">Battle of Trafalgar</a>, ending any plan to invade Britain. On 2 December 1805, Napoleon defeated a numerically superior Austro-Russian army at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Austerlitz</a>, forcing Austria's withdrawal from the coalition (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Pressburg_(1805)" title="Treaty of Pressburg (1805)">Treaty of Pressburg</a>) and dissolving the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. In 1806, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_Coalition" title="Fourth Coalition">Fourth Coalition</a> was set up. On 14 October Napoleon defeated the Prussians at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jena-Auerstedt" title="Battle of Jena-Auerstedt">Battle of Jena-Auerstedt</a>, marched through Germany and defeated the Russians on 14 June 1807 at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Friedland" title="Battle of Friedland">Friedland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_Tilsit" title="Treaties of Tilsit">Treaties of Tilsit</a> divided Europe between France and Russia and created the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw" title="Duchy of Warsaw">Duchy of Warsaw</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berezyna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="877" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/220px-Berezyna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/330px-Berezyna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/440px-Berezyna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berezyna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon's army at the retreat from Russia at the <a href="/wiki/Berezina" title="Berezina">Berezina</a> river</div></div></div>
<p>On 12 June 1812 Napoleon <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">invaded Russia</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e" title="Grande Armée">Grande Armée</a> of nearly 700,000 troops. After the measured victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Smolensk_(1812)" title="Battle of Smolensk (1812)">Smolensk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Borodino" title="Battle of Borodino">Borodino</a> Napoleon occupied Moscow, only to find it burned by the retreating Russian army. He was forced to withdraw. On the march back his army was harassed by <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossacks</a>, and suffered disease and starvation. Only 20,000 of his men survived the campaign. By 1813 the tide had begun to turn from Napoleon. Having been defeated by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sixth_Coalition" title="Sixth Coalition">seven nation army</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig" title="Battle of Leipzig">Battle of Leipzig</a> in October 1813, he was forced to abdicate after the <a href="/wiki/Six_Days%27_Campaign" title="Six Days' Campaign">Six Days' Campaign</a> and the occupation of Paris. Under the Treaty of Fontainebleau he was exiled to the island of <a href="/wiki/Elba" title="Elba">Elba</a>. He returned to France on 1 March 1815 (see <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days" title="Hundred Days">Hundred Days</a>), raised an army, but was finally defeated by a British and Prussian force at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a> on 18 June 1815 and exiled to the small British island of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> in the South Atlantic.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_the_French_Revolution">Impact of the French Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Europe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Impact of the French Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Influence of the French Revolution">Influence of the French Revolution</a></div>
<p>Roberts finds that the Revolutionary and Napoleonic wars, from 1793 to 1815, caused 4 million deaths (of whom 1 million were civilians); 1.4 million were French deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside France the Revolution had a major impact. Its ideas became widespread. Roberts argues that Napoleon was responsible for key ideas of the modern world, so that, "meritocracy, <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equality before the law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">property rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious toleration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secular_education" title="Secular education">modern secular education</a>, sound finances, and so on-were protected, consolidated, codified, and geographically extended by Napoleon during his 16 years of power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, the French armies in the 1790s and 1800s directly overthrew feudal remains in much of western Europe. They liberalised <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property laws</a>, ended <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">seigneurial dues</a>, abolished the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guild</a> of merchants and craftsmen to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a>, legalised divorce, closed the <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">Jewish ghettos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_emancipation" title="Jewish emancipation">made Jews equal to everyone else</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Inquisition</a> ended as did the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. The power of church courts and religious authority was sharply reduced and <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equality under the law</a> was proclaimed for all men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In foreign affairs, the French Army down to 1812 was quite successful. Roberts says that Napoleon fought 60 battles, losing only seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> France conquered Belgium and turned it into another province of France. It conquered the Netherlands, and made it a <a href="/wiki/Sister_republic" title="Sister republic">client state</a>. It took control of the German areas on the left bank of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine River</a> and set up a puppet <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a>. It conquered Switzerland and most of Italy, setting up a series of puppet states. The result was glory for France, and an infusion of much needed money from the conquered lands, which also provided direct support to the French Army. However the enemies of France, led by Britain and funded by the inexhaustible British Treasury, formed a <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition" title="War of the Second Coalition">Second Coalition</a> in 1799 (with Britain joined by Russia, the Ottoman Empire and Austria). It scored a series of victories that rolled back French successes, and trapped the French Army in Egypt. Napoleon himself slipped through the British blockade in October 1799, returning to Paris, where he overthrew the government and made himself the ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon conquered most of Italy in the name of the French Revolution in 1797–99. He consolidated old units and split up Austria's holdings. He set up a series of new republics, complete with new codes of law and abolition of old feudal privileges. Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Republic" title="Cisalpine Republic">Cisalpine Republic</a> was centered on Milan; Genoa became a republic; the Roman Republic was formed as well as the small <a href="/wiki/Ligurian_Republic" title="Ligurian Republic">Ligurian Republic</a> around Genoa. The <a href="/wiki/Parthenopean_Republic" title="Parthenopean Republic">Neapolitan Republic</a> was formed around Naples, but it lasted only five months. He later formed the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a>, with his brother as King. In addition, France turned the Netherlands into the <a href="/wiki/Batavian_Republic" title="Batavian Republic">Batavian Republic</a>, and Switzerland into the <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic Republic</a>. All these new countries were satellites of France, and had to pay large subsidies to Paris, as well as provide military support for Napoleon's wars. Their political and administrative systems were modernized, the metric system introduced, and trade barriers reduced. Jewish ghettos were abolished. Belgium and Piedmont became integral parts of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
